    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5999718476798746986">A spare yet eloquent, bittersweet yet inspiring story of a man and a woman who, in advanced age, come together to wrestle with the events of their lives and their hopes for the imminent future.<br/><br/>In the familiar setting of Holt, Colorado, home to all of Kent Haruf's inimitable fiction, Addie Moore pays an unexpected visit to a neighbor, Louis Waters. Her husband died years ag</span>
  <span id="freeText5999718476798746986" style="display:none">A spare yet eloquent, bittersweet yet inspiring story of a man and a woman who, in advanced age, come together to wrestle with the events of their lives and their hopes for the imminent future.<br /><br />In the familiar setting of Holt, Colorado, home to all of Kent Haruf's inimitable fiction, Addie Moore pays an unexpected visit to a neighbor, Louis Waters. Her husband died years ago, as did his wife, and in such a small town they naturally have known of each other for decades; in fact, Addie was quite fond of Louis's wife. His daughter lives hours away in Colorado Springs, her son even farther away in Grand Junction, and Addie and Louis have long been living alone in houses now empty of family, the nights so terribly lonely, especially with no one to talk with. <br /><br />Their brave adventures - their pleasures and their difficulties - are hugely involving and truly resonant, making Our Souls at Night the perfect final installment to this beloved writer's enduring contribution to American literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5999718476798746986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/418371-i-love-haruf-s-writing-but-his-fondness">I love Haruf&#39;s writing, but his fondness in later years for dialogue without quotation marks drove me crazy. Did an editor perhaps sneak them into this book after his death? Thanks in advance.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/3770266-henry-le-nav"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1334987138i/3770266._UX100_CR0,3,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/3770266-henry-le-nav">Henry Le Nav</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Indeed no quotation marks, and that drives me crazy too. But I found little of that "now wait who said what" that I have found in other books that emp<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Indeed no quotation marks, and that drives me crazy too. But I found little of that "now wait who said what" that I have found in other books that employ this tactic. <br /><br />The quality of this book was of such that I could forgive the lack of quotation marks. I loved the book. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1268850419?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2015</a>

        <a title="Julie Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Christine" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine">Julie Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=read-2015">read-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=usa-contemporary">usa-contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=best-of-2015">best-of-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=american-west">american-west</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1268850419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8115766802293643014">Kent Haruf wrote <i>Our Souls at Night</i> under a death sentence. Despite knowing his life was coming to a sooner end than it should, or perhaps because of this, he created a story about finding love in the final chapter. <i>Our Souls at Night</i> is simply, profoundly, an exquisite homage to love. And it broke my heart. Because, the story. Because, there will be no more. <br/><br/>I read <i>Our Souls at Night</i> in one sitting and wept in the final pages. Even now, thinking of endings, of the ending of this book, the endin</span>
  <span id="freeText8115766802293643014" style="display:none">Kent Haruf wrote <i>Our Souls at Night</i> under a death sentence. Despite knowing his life was coming to a sooner end than it should, or perhaps because of this, he created a story about finding love in the final chapter. <i>Our Souls at Night</i> is simply, profoundly, an exquisite homage to love. And it broke my heart. Because, the story. Because, there will be no more. <br /><br />I read <i>Our Souls at Night</i> in one sitting and wept in the final pages. Even now, thinking of endings, of the ending of this book, the ending of an author I adore, I weep. I weep for aging bodies, for small, mean minds that do such damage, for the elderly, forgotten and alone. I weep for all the stories we will never witness. And I come full circle, to mourn a cherished voice silenced by disease and death. Kent Haruf is the author I take to other countries, pressing his name into the ears of readers not familiar with his work. "<i>Plainsong</i>," I say, "<i>Eventide. Benediction</i>. Purity. Wisdom. Grace. An American voice like no other." <br /><br />And to this, I add the perfection of <i>Our Souls at Night</i>. <br /><br />Addie Moore and Louis Waters are neighbors, familiar but not friends, in Haruf's conjured Holt, CO. In their 70s, they are both widowed and living alone with their fractured pasts. One day, Addie approaches Louis and asks if he would consider coming to her house occasionally, to share her bed. <br /><blockquote>
  <i>I’m talking about getting through the night, she says. And lying warm in bed, companionably. Lying down in bed together and you staying the night. The nights are the worst. Don’t you think?<br /><br />Yes. I think so, he says.</i>
</blockquote> That's all you really need to know about the plot. What follows is achingly beautiful and gracious and solemn; a meditation on living in the present, a requiem to the held hand. <br /><br />My heart is so full of admiration and affection for Kent Haruf, his stories, his characters, his dry, dusty, flat eastern Colorado. I ache for what was, what will never be again. I am thankful for this final, perfect book—written for his wife and true love, Cathy—and her generosity in sharing it with her husband's readers. Kent, you are so missed. Thank you. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8115766802293643014" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1296579134" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1296579134" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1296579134?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=books-of-the-year-2015">books-of-the-year-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1296579134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6707088375937353459"><blockquote>
  <b>And then there was the day when Addie Moore made a call on Louis Waters.</b>
</blockquote>Louis and Addie are both getting on, in their 70s, Louis having lost his wife a year back, Addie a widow for some time. Both are lonely and could do with some company. While they have known each other for a long time, they have never been close. Acquaintances more than friends. Until Addie suggests that it would be a great help, given her trouble sleeping, if Louis would consent to sleep with her, not hide-the-salami sle</span>
  <span id="freeText6707088375937353459" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b>And then there was the day when Addie Moore made a call on Louis Waters.</b>
</blockquote>Louis and Addie are both getting on, in their 70s, Louis having lost his wife a year back, Addie a widow for some time. Both are lonely and could do with some company. While they have known each other for a long time, they have never been close. Acquaintances more than friends. Until Addie suggests that it would be a great help, given her trouble sleeping, if Louis would consent to sleep with her, not hide-the-salami sleep together, but sleep, and talk, in the same bed, overnight, companionship. Louis decides to give it a try. <br><br>Addie and Louis slowly begin to share their histories. The biddies of Holt, male and female, are taken aback, of course, at the presumed impropriety, as if, once elderly and alone, it was somehow sinful to still want to have a life. There are scenes in which they each are put on the spot and made to defend themselves to snickering locals about their arrangement. Feel free to cheer. Fueling his unhappiness with permanent rage about his childhood, Addie’s son, Gene, in particular, cannot tolerate his mother and Louis being together, projecting into it his fantasy that Louis is in the relationship to somehow swindle Addie out of her money. Problems ensue.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1435296718i/15329355.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Kent Haruf</b> - 1943-2014<br><br>A consistent focus in Haruf’s novels is the unconventional family, whether of elderly brothers taking in a pregnant girl, or grandparents taking care of an eight-year old. Well, that may not be all that unconventional these days, but it still ain’t Ozzie and Harriet. In this one, Addie’s son, Gene, has his hands full with problems at home, so sends his son, Jamie, to stay with Addie for a stretch of summer. Addie, Louis and Jamie form a very close relationship. There are moving sequences of outings and bonding moments that exude love and comfort, a contrast to the difficult relationships experienced between closer blood relations and spouses. Another Haruf concern is loneliness, at all ages. It is not only the raison d’etre of Louis and Addie’s arrangement, but is considered in relation to their former marital relationships. The loneliness of others comes in for some attention as well. Connections from generation to generation are considered. There are causes and effects, but life carries on. Haruf said, in relation to <i>Benediction</i><blockquote>
  <b> in the very next house, there is this 8-year-old girl who is the representative of hope and promise and youth and joy. And so what I am wanting people to feel is that the beginning and the ending in all of our lives are set side-by-side. They are not distinct from one another.</b>
</blockquote>The same could very well be applied here, connecting the lives of folks at both ends of their mortality. Haruf had been hoping to get to the January 2015 premier of the Denver Center production of a theater version of his novel <i>Benediction</i>. <br><br>The cast is much reduced in <i>Our Souls at Night</i> relative to that of his prior novels. The focus is on the two main characters, with Jamie in a large supporting role, and remaining there for the entirety. Of course their history brings in other players, but most remain off-stage or pop by for cameos. Addie and Louis trade stories each night in bed. It is a simple and effective mechanism for looking at two lives, their effect on others and others’ effects on them. Haruf used spare language, this, then that. If his writing were a font, it would be sans serif. And he is a master of showing instead of telling. After a rage-inducing encounter, <i> At home he went out to the garden and hoed for an hour, hard, almost violently…</i>. After a difficult scene, Haruf does not tell us how Louis feels. <i>There was a woman on the elevator, she looked at his face once and looked away.</i><br><br>His symbolism is also simple, and effective. The title refers not only to the time of day when Louis and Addie share their lives. It reminds us that time is short. A discussion about a nest of baby mice speaks to unpredictability. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506572383i/24028783._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Robert Redford and Jane Fonda as Louis and Addie</b> from the Netflix production - Image from Variety<br><br>In an interview Haruf did with John Moore for The Denver Center, he talks about his use of references to his own work in the novel:<blockquote>
  <b> Kent Haruf: … I will tell you there is a reference to the play <i>Benediction</i> in this new book. It's something these two old people have a little comment about.  <br><br>John Moore: That's part of the fun of reading of your stories. Even in <i>Benediction</i>, which features all new characters, there are those small references that reward those people who have been with you from the beginning. <br><br>Kent Haruf: It does. And it was a chance for me to have a little fun. Exactly as you say, people who know these other stories will immediately recognize what I am talking about.</b>
</blockquote> He sets his tale in Holt, Colorado, a place that will be familiar to readers of his earlier work. In another meta moment, his characters refer to the location in reference to seeing a play of a Kent Haruf story! (not Benediction) as a way of letting readers know about his usual locale<blockquote>
  <b> he took the physical details from Holt, the place names of the streets and what the country looks like and the location of things, but it’s not this town. And it’s not anybody in this town. All that’s made up. </b>
</blockquote>Well, of course Holt <i>is</i> fictitious but Haruf is making sure readers do not assign the place entirely to a single <i>real</i> location. I guess he wanted to clear that up before he left us.<blockquote>
<b>as a writer, I want to be thought of as somebody who had a very small talent but worked as best he could at using that talent. I want to think that I have written as close to the bone as I could. By that I mean that I was trying to get down to the fundamental, irreducible structure of life, and of our lives with one another. </b> - from the Denver Center interview</blockquote> I would disagree about the dimensions of his talent, but there is no question that Kent Haruf has offered the readers a world-view that may be bare bones in its form, but which is glorious in its realization. <br><br><i>Our Souls at Night</i>, his sixth novel, is the last book we will ever have from Kent Haruf. It is hopeful without being saccharine. Sharing love as darkness approaches may be one of love’s highest forms, offering no short term trade for a probably unrealistic long-term promise. It makes the sharing sweeter, in a way. I got the sense, without digging into specifics, that one thing Haruf was doing here was stopping off at some favorite spots in Holt for a final goodbye. Holt will remain available for generations of readers. Haruf passed away in November, 2014 at the age of 71. He will be missed.<br><br>Review posted – 6/26/15<br>Publication date – 5/16/15<br><br>=============================<b>EXTRA STUFF</b><br><br>Other Kent Haruf books we have enjoyed<br>-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/36474560" rel="nofollow noopener">Plainsong</a><br>-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/36474628" rel="nofollow noopener">Eventide</a><br>-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/619126024" rel="nofollow noopener">Benediction</a><br><br>Here is the complete <a href="http://www.denvercenter.org/blog-posts/news-center/2014/12/01/kent-haruf-the-complete-final-interview" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> Denver Center interview</a><br><br>In an <a href="http://www.identitytheory.com/kent-haruf/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> interview with Robert Birnbaum</a> for the Identity Theory site <br><br>In the <a href="http://www.penguinrandomhouse.com/books/252367/our-souls-at-night-by-kent-haruf/9781101875896/readers-guide/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> Reader’s Guide </a> of Random House’s page for the book, Haruf talks about how he worked:<blockquote>
  <b>The idea for the book has been floating around in my mind for quite a while. Now that I know I have, you know—a limited time—it was important to me to try to make good use of that time. So I went out there every day. Typically, I have always had a story pretty well plotted out before I start writing. This time I knew generally where the story was going, but I didn’t know very many of the details. So as it happened, I went out every day trusting myself to be able to add to the story each day. So I essentially wrote a new short chapter of the book every day. I’ve never had that experience before. I don’t want to get too fancy about it, but it was like something else was working to help me get this done. Call it a muse or spiritual guidance, I don’t know. All I know is that the trust I had in being able to write every day was helpful.” </b>
</blockquote>There is more info to be had on Haruf from <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/?title=Kent_Haruf" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">wikipedia</a> and <a href="http://www.barnesandnoble.com/writers/writerdetails.asp?z=y&amp;cid=1020577" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Barnes and Noble</a><br><br>The <a href="https://www.netflix.com/title/80104068" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Netflix film</a> first aired on September 29, 2017. It is a wonderful translation to film, capturing the essence of the book extraordinarily well, and with performances that are as wonderful as the written characters. <br><br>A couple more images from the film:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506572931i/24028809._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Image from La Biennale Di Venezia<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506572931i/24028810._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Image from The Times</span>
  <a data-text-id="6707088375937353459" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1272248685" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1272248685" />
    <a title="Ron Charles" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1384944-ron-charles"><img alt="Ron Charles" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567601289p2/1384944.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1272248685?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron Charles" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron Charles" href="/user/show/1384944-ron-charles">Ron Charles</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1384944-ron-charles?shelf=2015-favorites">2015-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1272248685" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15692793414197909044">Readers took their time finding Kent Haruf, but he was a patient man who didn’t care much for the trappings of fame anyhow. His popularity swelled quickly, though, when he published his third novel, “Plainsong,” in 1999 at the age of 56. The book was a bestseller for months and a finalist for the National Book Award, which meant he had to dress up for the ceremony in New York and wear a medal on a ribbon around his neck and feel genuinely uncomfortable. Afterward, he told the New York Times, “We</span>
  <span id="freeText15692793414197909044" style="display:none">Readers took their time finding Kent Haruf, but he was a patient man who didn’t care much for the trappings of fame anyhow. His popularity swelled quickly, though, when he published his third novel, “Plainsong,” in 1999 at the age of 56. The book was a bestseller for months and a finalist for the National Book Award, which meant he had to dress up for the ceremony in New York and wear a medal on a ribbon around his neck and feel genuinely uncomfortable. Afterward, he told the New York Times, “We’re nuts, crazy in this country about fame. We expect writers to be something between Hollywood starlet and a village idiot.”<br /><br />By the time he died last November at the age of 71, he had successfully avoided either of those fates and published five quiet, beloved novels about the people of Holt, Colo., a fictional town drawn from his itinerant adolescence. In his obituary, there was mention of a manuscript he’d completed just before dying, and now we have a chance to read that final book. Such posthumous publications come trailing clouds of skepticism, but “Our Souls at Night” is such a tender, carefully polished work that it seems a blessing we had no right to expect. . . . <br /><br /><i>To read the rest of this review, go to The Washington Post:</i><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/entertainment/books/kent-harufs-posthumous-novel-offers-a-tender-look-at-love-in-the-twilight/2015/05/19/b3d17bf8-f8e5-11e4-9030-b4732caefe81_story.html">http://www.washingtonpost.com/enterta...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15692793414197909044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3321688131" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3321688131" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3321688131?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer12943267825755757455">Engaging and touching. <br/><br/>A 70-year old widow in a small Colorado town goes over one day to a neighbor’s house. He’s a widower, a nice guy she knew from the days when she used to be good friends with his wife. She makes an audacious proposal: We’re both lonely; why don’t you come over at night and sleep with me - no sex - we’ll just talk. <br/><br/>It works.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1588779676i/29427067.jpg' width='400' height='300' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Of course the local gossips get wind of this, but at their age, neither of them cares. They decide to flaunt their relationship, going together to the bu</span>
  <span id="freeText12943267825755757455" style="display:none">Engaging and touching. <br><br>A 70-year old widow in a small Colorado town goes over one day to a neighbor’s house. He’s a widower, a nice guy she knew from the days when she used to be good friends with his wife. She makes an audacious proposal: We’re both lonely; why don’t you come over at night and sleep with me - no sex - we’ll just talk. <br><br>It works.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1588779676i/29427067.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Of course the local gossips get wind of this, but at their age, neither of them cares. They decide to flaunt their relationship, going together to the busiest lunch place in town at noon. <br><br>They engage in candid conversation, opening their souls to each other. Maybe they even talk of things they never told their spouses. They bare their souls about an affair, a child’s death, the death of their spouses, unfulfilled dreams.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1588779676i/29427068.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Meanwhile, the woman’s six-year old grandson moves in with her while his parents are working on repairing their marriage in a distant city. The three-some now make a family, spending all their time with the child as if they were all just starting out. They even get a dog. And they lived happily ever after.<br><br>No, they didn’t.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1588779676i/29427069._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="350" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This was my first book by this author who is best known for his Plainsong trilogy. (Eventide is #2 and Benediction #3). The author (1943-2014) set all his stories in the fictional town of Holt in eastern Colorado, actually based on Yuma, CO. Our Souls was his last work, finished the week before he died from a lung disease. In 2017 the book was made into a movie of the same name starring Robert Redford and Jane Fonda. <br><br>Top photo of Yuma, CO from coloradodirectory.com<br>Middle photo of Yuma by Loco Steve on flickr.com<br>The author from NY Times.com <br><br><br><br><br></span>

<div id="review_1120211629" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1120211629" />
    <a title="Angela M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4685500-angela-m"><img alt="Angela M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512600770p2/4685500.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1120211629?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela M" href="/user/show/4685500-angela-m">Angela M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781101875902" href="/book/show/23568220-our-souls-at-night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4685500-angela-m?shelf=edelweiss-reviews">edelweiss-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1120211629" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer477299133325859324"><br/>There are a few contemporary writers who will make a lasting mark and Kent Haruf is most certainly one of them . His quiet stories in their simplicity are meaningful , thoughtful stories reflecting the complexity of the human condition.<br/><br/> In his final novel , published posthumously, Haruf has given us another gift . I fell in love with this book from page one , the first sentence and then the perfect description of Cedar Street in Holt , Colorado the place where all his books take place . I fell i</span>
  <span id="freeText477299133325859324" style="display:none"><br />There are a few contemporary writers who will make a lasting mark and Kent Haruf is most certainly one of them . His quiet stories in their simplicity are meaningful , thoughtful stories reflecting the complexity of the human condition.<br /><br /> In his final novel , published posthumously, Haruf has given us another gift . I fell in love with this book from page one , the first sentence and then the perfect description of Cedar Street in Holt , Colorado the place where all his books take place . I fell in love with Louis Waters and Addie Moore and how they talk into the night about their lives and pasts , their transgressions , their hopes and desires , their losses , their families and how they bring joy to each other to ease the loneliness . I love how the title so perfectly reflects this. I love the humor and the honesty in their conversations. I love that Haruf could even make fun of himself as readers of other of his works will appreciate .<br /><br />It's a beautifully told story , sad yet uplifting and even sadder that this will be the last time we hear from this elegant writer . I'm sad it was such a short book . I will miss Louis and Addie's conversations. I'm not sure how I managed to miss reading one of his books but for some reason the only book of his that I haven't read is Where You once Belonged. I'm glad now that I have left myself one more . I've decided to save it for the next time I'm just not able to decide on what to read next . I'll read it then because I know it will be a good one .<br /><br />I am so grateful to Random House and Edelweiss for giving me the opportunity to read this ARC.</span>
  <a data-text-id="477299133325859324" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2039611300" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2039611300" />
    <a title="Jaline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline"><img alt="Jaline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530498058p2/7234171.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2039611300?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaline" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline">Jaline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7234171-jaline?shelf=xx2017-completed">xx2017-completed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7234171-jaline?shelf=x-favourites">x-favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7234171-jaline?shelf=wishlist-general">wishlist-general</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2039611300" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2633006658817348666">Only a few people can say they attended their grand-mother’s wedding. I am one of them. My Grannie was a widow for 15 years, met Gramps at a church outing and they married about 2 months after Grannie’s 70th birthday. Grannie was a joy in our lives – lots of stories, songs, and best of all for me, a wonderful correspondent. She was my most faithful pen pal for years. Gramps was about a year and half younger than Grannie and she would tease us about her marrying a ‘younger man’.<br/><br/>Gramps was a gentl</span>
  <span id="freeText2633006658817348666" style="display:none">Only a few people can say they attended their grand-mother’s wedding. I am one of them. My Grannie was a widow for 15 years, met Gramps at a church outing and they married about 2 months after Grannie’s 70th birthday. Grannie was a joy in our lives – lots of stories, songs, and best of all for me, a wonderful correspondent. She was my most faithful pen pal for years. Gramps was about a year and half younger than Grannie and she would tease us about her marrying a ‘younger man’.<br /><br />Gramps was a gentleman and a gentle man. I remember how he would tuck her arm in his and walk on the outside of the sidewalk; how he would always pull out her chair and seat her at meal times; how he was unfailingly polite, respectful, and loving toward her. Gramps didn’t drive, but Grannie drove until she was 90. Gramps would always open the driver’s door and ensure Grannie was settled before going around to the passenger’s seat. They had their 26th Wedding Anniversary in July and a couple of months later, Gramps died with Grannie following him in December. Our families loved them both and I still think of them and miss them at times.<br /><br />Reading Kent Haruf’s last novel, “Our Souls at Night”, was different yet it recalled to mind many aspects of Grannie and Gramps’ lives together. Addie Moore, 70, is a widow and Louis Waters, a widower. They have lived in the same small town for several decades and knew each other, their spouses, and their children.<br /><br />Addie and Louis are both lonely and they come together to talk, sharing their stories from the past, musing on the town’s people and events, and going on outings together. Their relationship is one that grows into something that is precious to both of them.<br /><br />Several things happen in the course of the story that draw them together. There are also forces at work trying to pry them apart – divided loyalties and family discord.<br /><br />The ending was poignant and sad in some respects, yet left room for hope. When in doubt, I reach for the hope every time – and a fistful of tissues.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2633006658817348666" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1273283441" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1273283441" />
    <a title="Cheri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106692-cheri"><img alt="Cheri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601402708p2/106692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1273283441?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheri" href="/user/show/106692-cheri">Cheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Cheri by: </span>
      Will Byrnes
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=colorado">colorado</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1273283441" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8175605109882552524">Growing up, the earliest years of my childhood, my paternal grandparents lived close by, my father was a pilot and was frequently gone for days, and I spent many days in my grandparent’s tiny caretaker apartment. I would follow my grandfather around while he polished the pews of the church, spruced up the grounds, sat by his side and “helped” him write his poetry, or just “sit a spell and ponder’ with him. With my grandmother I’d help piece her quilts together, or help her in their tiny kitchen </span>
  <span id="freeText8175605109882552524" style="display:none">Growing up, the earliest years of my childhood, my paternal grandparents lived close by, my father was a pilot and was frequently gone for days, and I spent many days in my grandparent’s tiny caretaker apartment. I would follow my grandfather around while he polished the pews of the church, spruced up the grounds, sat by his side and “helped” him write his poetry, or just “sit a spell and ponder’ with him. With my grandmother I’d help piece her quilts together, or help her in their tiny kitchen making cookies, dough for bread, baking bread. She would invariably send me home with a small ball of dough for my as a reminder that “love grows when you hold it in your open hands.” <br /><br />When they moved to Florida, I was crushed, but I still saw them several times a year. Not as often as weekly, but we wrote often. <br /><br />The year my grandmother died, my father called to tell me the news and I cried. Two days after she died, I got a letter in the mail. I recognized the shaky cursive handwriting at once and whatever composure I’d managed to grasp in that in-between time dissolved. There is nothing in that letter from my grandmother that was portentious, it was much the same as several other letters I’d received from her, how the oranges in their grove were doing, and so on… but these were her last words to me. I cherish them. <br /><br />I opened "Our Souls at Night” this last novel of Haruf’s, much the same way, with reverence for these last words. Haruf’s writing, as always, has a quiet gentleness and compassion with so much basic “goodness” that is rare, a treasure. In all of Haruf's novel, you must set aside the world and its busy-ness, and just sit a spell and ponder. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8175605109882552524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1450142549" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1450142549" />
    <a title="Glenn Sumi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28854579-glenn-sumi"><img alt="Glenn Sumi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444333567p2/28854579.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Sumi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Sumi" href="/user/show/28854579-glenn-sumi">Glenn Sumi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1450142549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1761382030260169398">You’d have to be stone-hearted not to respond to this beautiful little novel about late-in-life love and companionship.<br/><br/>In a small Colorado town, widowed neighbours Addie Moore and Louis Waters, who've known each other for decades, agree to share their nights together rather than sleep alone. This arrangement doesn’t involve sex, although gossiping neighbours assume it does; it’s more about sleeping in the same bed, talking, revealing stuff they don’t tell anyone else. <br/><br/>This is how Addie explains </span>
  <span id="freeText1761382030260169398" style="display:none">You’d have to be stone-hearted not to respond to this beautiful little novel about late-in-life love and companionship.<br /><br />In a small Colorado town, widowed neighbours Addie Moore and Louis Waters, who've known each other for decades, agree to share their nights together rather than sleep alone. This arrangement doesn’t involve sex, although gossiping neighbours assume it does; it’s more about sleeping in the same bed, talking, revealing stuff they don’t tell anyone else. <br /><br />This is how Addie explains her proposition to Louis: <br /><br /><blockquote>I mean we’re both alone. We’ve been by ourselves for too long. For years. I’m lonely. I think you might be too. I wonder if you would come and sleep in the night with me. And talk.<br />He stared at her, watching her, curious now, cautious.<br />You don’t say anything. Have I taken your breath away? she said.<br />I guess you have.<br />I’m not talking about sex.<br />I wondered.<br />No, not sex. I’m not looking at it that way. I think I’ve lost any sexual impulse a long time ago. I’m talking about getting through the night. And lying warm in bed, companionably. Lying down in bed together and you staying the night. The nights are the worst. Don’t you think?<br />Yes. I think so.</blockquote><br />Except for that awkward, five-syllable beaut “companionably,” this is terrific writing. <br /><br />Both have adult children from their marriages, and both have survived the big and little tragedies life has dealt them. As the nights pass and their defenses come down, they open up about their hopes, dreams and compromises, often withholding the really painful stuff until they’re ready. <br /><br />Meanwhile, they have to deal with the townsfolk spreading rumours and the grumblings from their children, who live hours away. Addie’s son is going through a separation and for a while her sad, insecure grandson comes to stay with her.<br /><br />The writing is exquisite: clear, funny, and unfussy, just like his characters. This was Kent Haruf’s last book, and you can sense him dispensing with artifice to get to the heart of a character and situation. Even the book’s opening lines get right to the point: <br /><br /><blockquote>And then there was the day when Addie Moore made a call on Louis Waters. It was an evening in May just before full dark.</blockquote><br />Simple. Folksy without being cloying, as if we’re mid conversation and this story just… <i>comes up</i>. <br /><br />I don’t want to say too much about the thin plot. But there are scenes that make my eyes water just thinking about them.<br /><br /><i>Our Souls At Night</i> is about living the examined life. It’s about reaching out and really communicating, not just existing, not just breathing. To hell with what people think. It’s your life. Live it. And while you’re at it, read this book.<br /><br />***<br /><br /><b>UPDATED Sep 9, 2016:</b>: This is going to be an original Netflix film starring <b>Jane Fonda</b> as Addie and <b>Robert Redford</b> as Louis! Also in the cast will be Bruce Dern (Dorlan), Judy Greer (Holly), Matthias Schoenaerts (Gene) and Iain Armitage, from Big Little Lies, as Addie's grandson Jamie. It's produced by one of the producers of the recent film Brooklyn, and directed by Ritesh Batra, who proved he knows how to handle a delicate love story in the film The Lunchbox.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1761382030260169398" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1877470080?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1877470080" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9003221166314849024"><br/>This novella by the author of <i>Plainsong</i> (1999) may be short, but it is large enough to contain two “souls” and enough of the small town of Holt, Colorado to bring it vividly to life.<br/><br/>The story is a simple one. One evening Addie Moore, a widow of seventy, makes a visit to widower Louis Walters—a longtime neighbor, but not someone she knows well—and makes him a “kind of proposal”: “I wonder if you would come and sleep in the night with me and talk.” It’s not about sex, she adds—she thinks she lost </span>
  <span id="freeText9003221166314849024" style="display:none"><br />This novella by the author of <i>Plainsong</i> (1999) may be short, but it is large enough to contain two “souls” and enough of the small town of Holt, Colorado to bring it vividly to life.<br /><br />The story is a simple one. One evening Addie Moore, a widow of seventy, makes a visit to widower Louis Walters—a longtime neighbor, but not someone she knows well—and makes him a “kind of proposal”: “I wonder if you would come and sleep in the night with me and talk.” It’s not about sex, she adds—she thinks she lost the impulse long ago—but about “getting through the night,” about “lying warm in bed, companionably.”<br /> <br />The next night, Louis takes her up on her offer. And so begins the story of two “souls at night,” sharing their histories, their fears, and simple affection. Their lives, however, soon become complicated, what with nosy neighbors, a visiting daughter, a small grandson, and a son who thoroughly disapproves.<br /><br />As we watch Addie and Louis move closer to one another, through night time conversations and day time adventures, we grow to care about their friendship and how it moves gently toward love. <br /><br />The ending, though surprising, is perhaps inevitable. But I had difficulty reading it through my tears.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18251397765488569151">This is an absolutely beautiful story of two people who give comfort to each late in life. I laughed and I cried! I just loved this novel! It is so well written and so lovely!<br/><br/>Louis and Addy are living in Holt, Colorado. They've known each other for many many years. One day 70 year old Addy approaches Louis and asks him if he will come stay with her at night to sleep with her. This is not a sexual thing. Addy is having trouble sleeping. Louis ends up going along with this. <br/><br/>I loved the stories tha</span>
  <span id="freeText18251397765488569151" style="display:none">This is an absolutely beautiful story of two people who give comfort to each late in life. I laughed and I cried! I just loved this novel! It is so well written and so lovely!<br /><br />Louis and Addy are living in Holt, Colorado. They've known each other for many many years. One day 70 year old Addy approaches Louis and asks him if he will come stay with her at night to sleep with her. This is not a sexual thing. Addy is having trouble sleeping. Louis ends up going along with this. <br /><br />I loved the stories that they told to each other, including their tragedies that made me cry. I also loved Addie's curious Grandson and the dog that they got him. The neighbors gossiped, but that didn't keep them apart. <br /><br />I listened to the the audio version of this book. It is exquisitely narrated by Mark Bramhall. This is a short book. It is only three and half hours long, but there is a lot of meaning and substance to it.<br /><br />I'm ashamed to say that I have never read a Kent Haruf novel. Since he has passed on, I will have to start reading some of his older works.<br /><br />Highly recommended to everyone who has yet to read it. I am late to the party again, but I'm so happy that I went! I have a feeling these characters are going to stick with me for a very long time.</span>

<div id="review_1311167406" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1311167406" />
    <a title="Candi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3639005-candi"><img alt="Candi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585187464p2/3639005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1311167406?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Candi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Candi" href="/user/show/3639005-candi">Candi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=contemporary-literary">contemporary-literary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1311167406" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14995896030734326474"><i>"I do love this physical world. I love this physical life with you. And the air and the country. The backyard, the gravel in the back alley. The grass. The cool nights. Lying in bed talking with you in the dark."</i><br/> <br/>These simple yet sublime words expressed between two lonely souls managed to evoke such a feeling of peace in me. Addie and Louis, both widowed for some time, wish only to spend the remainder of their lives in companionship and quiet pleasure. What more can we really ask for in this lif</span>
  <span id="freeText14995896030734326474" style="display:none"><i>"I do love this physical world. I love this physical life with you. And the air and the country. The backyard, the gravel in the back alley. The grass. The cool nights. Lying in bed talking with you in the dark."</i><br /> <br />These simple yet sublime words expressed between two lonely souls managed to evoke such a feeling of peace in me. Addie and Louis, both widowed for some time, wish only to spend the remainder of their lives in companionship and quiet pleasure. What more can we really ask for in this life that is sometimes filled with hardships and heartache? <br /><br />This is my second Kent Haruf novel and there is no doubt in my mind that this gifted man understood people. With a straightforward yet elegant style, Haruf manages to convey the heart of his characters. Even in this short piece of fiction we discern the innermost hopes, fears and desires of Addie and Louis. I adored Addie's little grandson - his love for his dog and his curiosity about the baby mice. I thought their neighbor, Ruth, would be just another busybody but came to appreciate her clever advice to Addie – she managed to make me laugh! And then there were the gossips and the "bucket dippers" as my daughter would say. Why can't folks just leave well enough alone? Addie's son Carl with his heaping pile of baggage grated on my last nerve and I simply could not grasp why he was so opposed to Louis and Addie's gentle and unimposing relationship. I won't give away the last 4 sentences of this little gem, but let me just say they were quite poignant and clearly bittersweet. <br /> <br />I highly recommend <b>Our Souls at Night</b> and Haruf's <b>Plainsong</b> which I liked even better than this. They will make you think about humanity and those things that matter the most to you. Hearing about catastrophes and senseless killings, I want to stop and think about living each day the best that I can and appreciating the basic and happiest things in life – my family, long summer nights, the tinkling of the wind chimes outside my door, my daughter's extra kisses and "neck" hugs, the sound of my son whistling, the taste of root beer floats, and my teetering stack of books, just to name a few of those things for which I am most grateful. <br />4.5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="14995896030734326474" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Katie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56035852-katie"><img alt="Katie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469958232p2/56035852.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer18379581198191979001">As you’d expect from an author who knows he is dying this is a tremendously wise novel about the pursuit of everyday happiness and all the petty and ingrained obstructions such a pursuit can meet when it’s unconventional. Some of the things he did really well – showed us how much responsibility we have as parents but also how many handicaps, because it’s virtually impossible to escape from stifling generational patterns of behaviour; showed us how similar a censorious view of unconventional rela</span>
  <span id="freeText18379581198191979001" style="display:none">As you’d expect from an author who knows he is dying this is a tremendously wise novel about the pursuit of everyday happiness and all the petty and ingrained obstructions such a pursuit can meet when it’s unconventional. Some of the things he did really well – showed us how much responsibility we have as parents but also how many handicaps, because it’s virtually impossible to escape from stifling generational patterns of behaviour; showed us how similar a censorious view of unconventional relationships is to racism or homophobia and all other forms of bigoted mean-spirited social hostility; showed us how much poetry there is in the everyday if we choose to live with a little bit of courage. And, on top of that, he showed what a transfiguring effect the presence of a dog can have on a child’s life! This was so easy and such a delight to read. </span>

His voice was so gentle and soothing!<br /><br />I don't think I can say anymore or anything better than Brenda......so let's just call it a "sister read".<br /><br />BRENDA'S REVIEW BELOW:<br /><br />Our Souls at Night is a simple, graceful and tender story of elderly Louis and Addie who while seeking companionship share a touching relationship, quietly at night, sharing their stories of their past, happiness, missed opportunities, mistakes and grief. I love their quiet acceptance of each other and how they start to enjoy the quiet and simple pleasures in life until the noise and judgment of others threaten to destroy it. <br /><br />Listening to this was a special one for me and I became emerged into their lives that left me feeling peaceful and hopeful with the possibility that with change and growth there is quiet later in life. <br /><br />Our Souls at Night is a beautiful novella, short and quick that will have me thinking of the beauty in it for a long time.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1665330147" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1665330147" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1665330147?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9781447299370" href="/book/show/27807379-our-souls-at-night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=ageing-and-old-age">ageing-and-old-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=usa-and-canada">usa-and-canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1665330147" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1560012384681962938"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479385428i/21183503.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>“<i>We're still talking. For as long as we can. For as long as it lasts.</i>”<br/> <br/>To paraphrase, <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_medium_is_the_message' rel='nofollow noopener'>Marshall McLuhan</a>, to what extent is the medium the message?<br/><br/>Before I read this, and for most of the time I was reading it, I thought it was about the visceral human need for communication and company. And it is. But as I approached the end, I realised it was at least as much about how the emotional impact and engagement of conversations are affected by the medium used.<br/><br/><b>Situation (not Plot)</b><br/><br/>Addie (70) and Louis (si</span>
  <span id="freeText1560012384681962938" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479385428i/21183503.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>“<i>We're still talking. For as long as we can. For as long as it lasts.</i>”<br> <br>To paraphrase, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_medium_is_the_message" rel="nofollow noopener">Marshall McLuhan</a>, to what extent is the medium the message?<br><br>Before I read this, and for most of the time I was reading it, I thought it was about the visceral human need for communication and company. And it is. But as I approached the end, I realised it was at least as much about how the emotional impact and engagement of conversations are affected by the medium used.<br><br><b>Situation (not Plot)</b><br><br>Addie (70) and Louis (similar) are lonely neighbours who, at her suggestion, start spending the night together, for companionship and especially to talk. Addie and Louis discuss their pasts (they don’t know each other very well): joys, heartbreaks, the ordinary, and the many things they wish they had done differently. <br><br>“<i>It’s a kind of mystery… Being here in the dark of night. The talking. Hearing you breathe next to me if I wake up.</i>”<br><br>Despite initial discretion, neighbours notice, gossip, and affect shock, while Addie and Louis’ adult children are embarrassed and angry. That’s it.<br><br><b>The Medium is the Message?</b><br><br>Our interactions vary, even with the same person, according to the medium (as well as the context and relationship): across the dinner table, down the phone, via instant messaging, email, or snail mail. Sign language would be different again.<br><br>These issues are key in Addie and Louis’ relationships, past and present, but it was only towards the end that I realised it, along with the earlier significance of Jamie’s attachment to his phone.<br><br>It’s easy to replace quality with quantity: obsessive texting, rather than living in the present and engaging with those directly in front of us. (Or perhaps texting those directly in front of us!) It’s only when forced to rely on a more detached medium that we fully realise what we have lost.<br><br>As the parent of a twenty-something at university, I miss the quotidian, casual conversations that arose in a shared home. I rejoice at the wonder of technology, even as I mourn the veil of detachment inherent in it. In theory, we’re constantly available, but available for what?<br><br><b>Skin Hunger</b><br><br>Touch feels at least as important as words: a touch is worth a thousand pictures (so, a million words?!). But the wrong touch, even if not physically painful, can be more agonising than the thrill of the intimate touches we crave from those we love.<br><br>“<i>I just want to live simply and pay attention to what’s happening each day.</i>”<br>Touch is not a motive for Addie and Louis, but the tangibility of the world is important to them. When Louis first comes over, he needs to see the whole house, to know where he is, physically. Later, they discuss how much they love what is around them and the aspects of life they share: “<i>And the air and the country. The backyard, the gravel in the back alley. The grass. The cool nights. Lying in bed talking with you in the dark.</i>” Simple pleasures. But important. It’s a way of being grounded in the moment - all the more poignant because they are conscious that moments are slipping away.<br><br>Sex is not a motive either, even though they are fairly young old people. That’s fine for them as individuals, just as long as it doesn’t feed into the erroneous assumption that older people in general don’t have or even want much sex. (If Haruf had made them older, the camping trip might have stretched credulity, it would have been unusual to have such a young grandson, and tricky for friend Ruth to be almost a generation older than them.) <br><br><b>Family Obligations</b><br><br>Families are at the heart of this story: the one we are born into, the ones we create or drift into, the ones we escape, and the alternative groupings we sometimes forge to fill the void created by loss, disappointment, boredom, or betrayal. When one’s family is flawed, the desire to build something better is overwhelming, even if it is impossible to achieve.<br><br>There are all forms of abandonment here, but very little self-pity: loss of parents, partners, siblings, and children, lost by death or walking away. Even the dog is from a rescue shelter, and some baby mice are abandoned by their mother, to the anxiety of a boy abandoned by his own mother. These people don’t suppress their pain, but they don’t indulge it either. They look for pragmatic solutions - such as sharing a bed with a near stranger for company, although if Addie read the BBC News website today, she might have considered an inter-generational befriending scheme instead, such as one of those described <a href="http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-37976567" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>.<br><br>Addie and Louis have to figure out how much they should worry about, let alone be constrained by what other people think. It can be fun to challenge town busybodies, but family are another matter.<br><br>“<i>I am behaving. I’m doing what I want and it isn’t hurting anyone.</i>”<br>What do we owe our family in terms of outward respectability, and how much freedom do they owe us? Parents may have firm expectations of their children, but even if they are fair and reasonable ones, should those children hold their parents to similar, or higher, standards?<br><br>Letting go of one’s children is hard, but it is usually a positive step towards the autonomy of adulthood. Letting go of one’s parents will be harder. But before that, is the possibility of the opposite: parenting one’s parents, their declining independence reigning in one’s own. I strive not to leverage my increasing role in my parents’ lives to limit their freedom and choices beyond what is essential, but it’s tricky - especially defining “essential”. <br><br><b>A Plain Song at Eventide</b><br><br>This book opens, mid-thought, “And then there was the day when…”, which is apt, as conversational backstory fills many of the pages.<br><br>It has the plain language of Plainsong (my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1445811708" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>) and Eventide (my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1453096387" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>), and is set in the same town, at the eventide of Addie and Louis’ lives (and written at Haruf’s), but it rarely attains quite the same heights of poetic simplicity. That helps eschew the sentimentality it could have succumbed to.<br><br>Here, the poignancy is in the situation, rather than the language. A situation infused with tender beauty and quiet pain, balanced by tentative, hopeful grasps of happiness.<br><br><b>Fiction within Fiction</b><br><br>All Haruf’s novels are set in the fictional town of Holt, Colorado, and some share characters. In this, Addie and Louis discuss the possibility of seeing a stage production of Haruf’s best-known novel, Plainsong, but Louis is not keen, as he doesn’t think it very realistic. <br><br>“He could write a book about us”, suggests Addie. Well, he did. And it’s beautiful. I hope Louis doesn’t mind; he told Addie, “I don’t want to be in any book”, but I’m glad that he is. I believed in Plainsong, but I believe in Addie and Louis even more. <br><br><b>Other Quotes</b><br><br>•	“The stars were all shining and there were the farmlights and yardlights all looking blue in the dark, Everything looking normal, except nothing was normal anymore, everything was at some kind of cliff’s edge.”<br><br>•	“Outside the dark bedroom suddenly the wind came up and blew hard in the open windows whipping the curtains back and forth. Then it started to rain… Doesn’t it smell lovely.”<br><br>•	“Louis put his hand out of the open window and caught the rain dripping off the eaves and came to bed and touched his wet hand on Addie’s soft cheek.”<br> <br>•	“We had that long time of joined life, even if it wasn’t good for either one of us. That was our history.”<br><br>•	Slight spoiler <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[: “It was not the same lighthearted pleasure and discovery. And gradually there were nights when he stayed home… They still held each other… more out of habit and desolation and anticipated loneliness and disheartenment, as if they were trying to store up the moments together against what was coming.” <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>•	The ending was not quite as I expected, and all the better for that. Slight spoiler <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[: After a forced and probably permanent separation, they talk on the phone: “It's like when we started… Except we're careful now... We're still talking. For as long as we can. For as long as it lasts.” <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><br>For those who are distracted by such things (Apatt, for one), there are no quotation marks to indicate dialogue. It’s not confusing, and is integral to the plain aesthetic, but minimalism isn’t for everyone. <br><br>Image source for “the medium is message” and open book:<br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.brafton.com/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/MEDMESS-350x222.jpeg">http://www.brafton.com/wp-content/upl...</a></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2121156645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18110398707566883042">What a lovely little book!<br/><br/>Simply put, this book is about two people of advancing age who are lonely, and make the decision to develop a bond and provide each other with comfort to get through the nights alone. Louis spends the night at Addie's home, and in doing so they grow to be close and dependent upon one another for emotional strength.<br/><br/>Both are widowed, and live in the same small town of Holt, CO. This is important because part of the book deals with nosy busybodies who can't mind their own </span>
  <span id="freeText18110398707566883042" style="display:none">What a lovely little book!<br /><br />Simply put, this book is about two people of advancing age who are lonely, and make the decision to develop a bond and provide each other with comfort to get through the nights alone. Louis spends the night at Addie's home, and in doing so they grow to be close and dependent upon one another for emotional strength.<br /><br />Both are widowed, and live in the same small town of Holt, CO. This is important because part of the book deals with nosy busybodies who can't mind their own beeswax. <br /><br />I found myself choking up during parts of this book (which is all too short, btw!), especially during the ending. I would have actually given this book 5 stars had it not been for the ending, but I understand that such is life and sometimes people make choices that I wouldn't make. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ I would have told her son to learn his place! Go pound sand, you adult brat! She supports him financially, yet he chases away one of the only good things she has going for herself. He has a lot of nerve, and my jaw dropped when I read how her son Gene was keeping Addie's grandson away from her just to chase away Louis. I thought it was very telling how Gene was mostly concerned about his inheritance and had zero problem with making his sweet little son cry with heartbreak over not being able to talk to his grandmother. I bet that jerk would have changed his tune real fast if she said "No grandson, no check. How do ya like them apples now, boy?" Ugh, I can't stand Gene!!!  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The similarities to Cormac McCarthy's sparse writing style and (almost) run-on sentences were glaringly obvious here. I had no problem with it, because you know the old saying: imitation is the sincerest form of flattery. I think McCarthy would give his stamp of approval on this deeply moving story. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18110398707566883042" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4618089078760048509">Oh dear. This book and I just aren’t a good fit. Books keep teaching me things about what I like and don’t like. This book taught me I just don't do quiet, simplistic, low-key. I guess I'm just a drama queen, liking my stories over the top. And I apparently don't like small town settings. Give me city, give me nuts! I like complicated over simplistic, no doubt because I make everything way too complicated :)<br/><br/>I had a problem with the premise: I had trouble believing anyone would invite a stranger </span>
  <span id="freeText4618089078760048509" style="display:none">Oh dear. This book and I just aren’t a good fit. Books keep teaching me things about what I like and don’t like. This book taught me I just don't do quiet, simplistic, low-key. I guess I'm just a drama queen, liking my stories over the top. And I apparently don't like small town settings. Give me city, give me nuts! I like complicated over simplistic, no doubt because I make everything way too complicated :)<br /><br />I had a problem with the premise: I had trouble believing anyone would invite a stranger to bed instead of to coffee. Bed seems too personal a space to share with the unfamiliar. Would anyone seriously invite a stranger to share snoring, restlessness, bumping, sleep machines, and all the other embarrassing sounds and sights that are part of the night? And would anyone accept? I know I'm just analyzing it too much. But this is realistic fiction and I just don’t find the premise realistic.<br /><br />The idea of committing to learn about a stranger is intriguing, maybe, say, if they sat at a table and had some coffee cups, as barriers, between them. <br /><br />Once I suspend disbelief, it's not a bad little story. I know and appreciate that this book is near and dear to many, but it's just not my cup of tea. Or coffee.<br /></span>
